</p><p>Media outlets noted similarities between the sleeve covers of Lil' Kim's <i>Hard Core</i> and Brown's <i><a href="/wiki/Ill_Na_Na" title="Ill Na Na">Ill Na Na</a>,</i> where the two were shown wearing the same outfit.<sup id="cite_ref-vibe2_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vibe2-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> In 1997, the feud led to the deterioration of their friendship, and resulted in the cancellation of a collaborative album <i>Thelma &amp; Louise.</i> Brown noted that the breakdown of their relationship was influenced by their conflicting entourages.<sup id="cite_ref-vibe_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vibe-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> They first attempted a reconciliation in 1998 after Lil' Kim called Brown and her mother after the two were held at gunpoint during a house burglary.<sup id="cite_ref-vibe_171-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vibe-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mtv_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mtv-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 1999, critics noted the track "My Life" from Brown's second studio effort <i><a href="/wiki/Chyna_Doll_(album)" title="Chyna Doll (album)">Chyna Doll</a></i> as being an "open appeal" to their friendship. In the song, Brown describes their relationship being "lost for pride." Later in the album, however, Brown attacks "mistresses." As music journalist Evelyn McDonnell noted, this seemed pointed at Lil' Kim.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-villagevoice_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-villagevoice-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Critics thought the records were a subtle diss to Lil' Kim, especially since during this time Brown had supported Lil' Kim's rival, <a href="/wiki/Faith_Evans" title="Faith Evans">Faith Evans</a>, in multiple interviews.<sup id="cite_ref-villagevoice_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-villagevoice-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> In February 1999, some music critics noted that Brown "suddenly sounded 'exactly' like Lil' Kim."<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In late May 1999, <a href="/wiki/Lil%27_Cease" title="Lil&#39; Cease">Lil' Cease</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Play_Around" title="Play Around">Play Around</a>," featuring Lil' Kim and Diddy, was released and featured on Cease's debut <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wonderful_World_of_Cease_A_Leo" title="The Wonderful World of Cease A Leo">The Wonderful World of Cease A Leo</a>.</i><sup id="cite_ref-bet_173-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bet-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> In the song, after Lil' Kim's guest rap, Diddy raps: "Stop trying to sound like her too bitches"; a diss aimed at Foxy.<sup id="cite_ref-bet_173-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bet-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> The line spurred enough controversy that a writer remarked, "It's finally coming out. Foxy Brown bites worse than a pit bull."<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, Lil' Kim's guest appearance on <a href="/wiki/Mobb_Deep" title="Mobb Deep">Mobb Deep</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Quiet_Storm_(song)" title="Quiet Storm (song)">Quiet Storm (Remix)</a>" and her second album's title track, "The Notorious K.I.M.," were both released as diss records aimed at Foxy Brown.<sup id="cite_ref-bet_173-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bet-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Angered by Lil' Kim's response, Brown collaborated with longtime associates <a href="/wiki/Capone-N-Noreaga" title="Capone-N-Noreaga">Capone-N-Noreaga</a> on the track, "Bang, Bang."<sup id="cite_ref-bet_173-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bet-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> In the track, Brown mimicked Lil' Kim's interpretation of <a href="/wiki/MC_Lyte" title="MC Lyte">MC Lyte</a>'s <a href="/wiki/10%25_Dis" title="10% Dis">10% Dis</a>, and went on to recite: "You and Diddy y'all kill me with that subliminal shit."<sup id="cite_ref-bet_173-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bet-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Towards the end of her verse, Brown attacked Lil' Kim's grieving for the loss of Biggie Smalls by remarking: "Let the nigga [Biggie] rest in peace, and hop off his dick, bitch do you."<sup id="cite_ref-bet_173-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bet-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bangbang_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bangbang-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On February 26, 2001, at 3 p.m., as Lil' Kim left New York radio station Hot 97, over twenty shots were fired between two groups of three men.<sup id="cite_ref-shootingrattles_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shootingrattles-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> One of the men, Capone of Capone-N-Noreaga, was entering the Hot 97 building promote <a href="/wiki/DJ_Clue%3F" class="mw-redirect" title="DJ Clue?">DJ Clue</a>'s new album, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Professional_2" title="The Professional 2">The Professional 2</a></i> (which happened to have also featured Lil' Kim).<sup id="cite_ref-capone-n-noreaga_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-capone-n-noreaga-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> An affiliate, Efrain Ocasio from Capone's entourage, was shot in the back; both Lil' Kim's and Capone's groups denied any involvement in the shooting.<sup id="cite_ref-capone-n-noreaga_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-capone-n-noreaga-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> However, detectives later informed <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_News_(New_York)" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily News (New York)">The New York Daily News</a></i> that it was a result of the verses Foxy Brown recited in "Bang, Bang."<sup id="cite_ref-capone-n-noreaga_186-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-capone-n-noreaga-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shaken up by the incident, Brown tried to reach out to Lil' Kim for a truce.<sup id="cite_ref-truce_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-truce-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Brown stated, "I really don't know how it started. But Russell [Simmons] and I, we got together, and I said, 'Russell, I want to call a truce.' I want to have a sit-down with Kim. I don't care what it is. Let's just end it. We can even do a collaboration. We're bigger than this. If it has to start with me, let it start with me."<sup id="cite_ref-truce_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-truce-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Brown extended an olive branch to Lil' Kim's camp, however Lil' Kim had cut all ties with Diddy and Bad Boy associates and wanted no communication with Brown whatsoever.<sup id="cite_ref-truce_187-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-truce-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> In March 2005, Lil' Kim was convicted of lying about the shooting to a federal grand jury.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> On July 6, 2005, she was sentenced to prison for three counts of perjury and one count of conspiracy.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the four years before Lil' Kim's sentencing, she and Brown exchanged subtle diss records, among them Lil' Kim's <i>La Bella Mafia,</i><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> "Quiet,"<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> and "Guess Who's Back";<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> and in turn, Brown's <i><a href="/wiki/Ill_Na_Na_2:_The_Fever" title="Ill Na Na 2: The Fever">Ill Na Na 2: The Fever</a></i> and various mixtape freestyles.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> In the midst of these diss recordings, <a href="/wiki/Doug_Banks" title="Doug Banks">Doug Banks</a> interviewed Brown in 2003, hoping she would disclose further details pertaining to their dispute.<sup id="cite_ref-dougbanks_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dougbanks-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> Brown claimed that Lil' Kim was allegedly jealous that Biggie was to include Brown in his Junior M.A.F.I.A. collective.<sup id="cite_ref-dougbanks_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dougbanks-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> Brown also added that a tell-all book disclosing the feud would be released for Christmas 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-dougbanks_196-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dougbanks-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> In her final comments on the dispute, Brown stated: "Kim is the only female artist that keeps me on my toes. She's the only one that I can look at; and any other artist that says they don't have that one person that keeps them driven... is lying."<sup id="cite_ref-dougbanks_196-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dougbanks-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following her release from prison, Lil' Kim no longer acknowledged Brown.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> Brown, on the other hand, has consistently targeted Lil' Kim in her music and concert venues since the latter's prison release.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On May 17, 2012, Lil' Kim gave an interview to a radio show, <i>The Breakfast Club.</i><sup id="cite_ref-breakfast_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-breakfast-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> When asked about whether or not she had spoken to Brown at all in recent years, she replied, "I don't even know her. And when I say that; I don't know who she is to these days. I wouldn't even know what her voice sounds like."<sup id="cite_ref-breakfast_204-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-breakfast-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nicki_Minaj">Nicki Minaj</span></h3>
<p>Lil' Kim has been embroiled in a feud with fellow rapper <a href="/wiki/Nicki_Minaj" title="Nicki Minaj">Nicki Minaj</a> since Minaj's success with <i><a href="/wiki/Pink_Friday" title="Pink Friday">Pink Friday</a>.</i><sup id="cite_ref-bfdiss_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bfdiss-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> With Lil' Kim and media critics noticing resemblances with her on the <i>Sucka Free</i> mixtape,<sup id="cite_ref-NYDailyNews_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYDailyNews-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> Lil' Kim accused Minaj of copying her image saying, "If you are going to steal my swag, you gonna have to pay. Something gotta give. You help me, I help you. That's how it goes to me."<sup id="cite_ref-NYDailyNews_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYDailyNews-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> Minaj's single "<a href="/wiki/Roman%27s_Revenge" title="Roman&#39;s Revenge">Roman's Revenge</a>" with <a href="/wiki/Eminem" title="Eminem">Eminem</a> was believed by critics to be a response to Lil' Kim's comments,<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> although at the time she denied this.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Minaj responded to the situation in an interview on The Angie Martinez show, saying "She picked a fight with Foxy, then she picked a fight with <a href="/wiki/Eve_(rapper)" title="Eve (rapper)">Eve</a>, then she picked a fight with <a href="/wiki/Remy_Ma" title="Remy Ma">Remy</a>, then it was Mrs. Wallace, then it was Nicki Minaj. Every time you in the news, it's 'cause you gettin' at somebody! Where's your music? Put your music out, and when I see your name on <i>Billboard,</i> that's when I'll respond to you. Other than that, goodbye. It's Barbie, bitch."<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lil' Kim reignited the feud following the release of her mixtape <i>Black Friday,</i> an album that directly referenced Nicki Minaj, via her debut album title and freestyling on Minaj's songs such as "<a href="/wiki/Did_It_On%27em" title="Did It On&#39;em">Did It On'em</a>" (that became "Pissin' On'em"). The mixtape cover featured artwork that showed Lil' Kim decapitating Minaj with a sword.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> A fragment of Minaj's <a href="/wiki/Diss_track" class="mw-redirect" title="Diss track">diss track</a> "Tragedy" was released online in April 2011, featuring lyrics "Pink Friday, Eminem, 8 Mile/ It must hurt to sell your album on PayPal."<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> Following the release of Minaj's second album, <i><a href="/wiki/Pink_Friday:_Roman_Reloaded" title="Pink Friday: Roman Reloaded">Pink Friday: Roman Reloaded</a>,</i> critics suggested that the tracks "<a href="/wiki/Stupid_Hoe" title="Stupid Hoe">Stupid Hoe</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> may have contained attacks at Lil' Kim. Lil' Kim later suggested in an interview with Power105's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Breakfast_Club_(radio_show)" title="The Breakfast Club (radio show)">Breakfast Club</a></i> that the song "<a href="/wiki/Pink_Friday:_Roman_Reloaded" title="Pink Friday: Roman Reloaded">Automatic</a>" from Minaj's second album was similar to her unreleased material, also calling Minaj "obnoxious" and "catty."<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a 2018 interview with <a href="/wiki/Genius_(website)" class="mw-redirect" title="Genius (website)">Genius</a>, Minaj alluded to the situation with Lil' Kim, seemingly confirming that the track "Roman's Revenge" was about the feud, saying, "Someone got at me. A veteran got at me, and I hit them with 'Roman's Revenge' and then I kept on going."<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> In 2021, Lil' Kim stated on the red carpet of the <a href="/wiki/BET_Awards" title="BET Awards">BET Awards</a> that she would like to do a <a href="/wiki/Verzuz" title="Verzuz">Verzuz</a> with Minaj.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:30%; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>The idea of female rappers owning their sexuality is nothing new in the modern hip hop landscape. However, this wasn't always the case, and there's arguably one woman to thank -- Lil' Kim<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... Before she came out, it was argued that sexy women in hip hop were exploited by the industry, reduced to mere video girls cast to dance around the male breadwinner. Kim reversed this notion, announcing her arrival to hip hop with a provocative promo poster that saw her clad in a leopard print bikini and matching feather-trimmed robe.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">—<i><a href="/wiki/I-D" title="I-D">i-D</a></i> writer Jake Hall.<sup id="cite_ref-id_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-id-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div><p> Several media outlets have referred to Lil' Kim as the "<a href="/wiki/Honorific_nicknames_in_popular_music#L" title="Honorific nicknames in popular music">Queen of Rap</a>,"<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> and by the alias she uses, "Queen Bee."<sup id="cite_ref-newsweek_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newsweek-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbc_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CBS_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CBS-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Newsweek" title="Newsweek">Newsweek</a></i> has called her the "First Lady of Rap," with editor Allison Samuels writing in 2000 that she has "transcended the male-dominated world of rap to become one of America's sassiest, most engaging icons."<sup id="cite_ref-newsweek_219-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newsweek-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lil' Kim's music catalog features undertones of <a href="/wiki/Sex-positive_feminism" title="Sex-positive feminism">sex-positive feminism</a> and the importance of female pleasure, instead of the man as the "dominant priority," through explicit lyrics about sex.<sup id="cite_ref-id_216-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-id-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Dazed" title="Dazed">Dazed</a></i> considered her "the first high-profile female rapper to flip the script on <a href="/wiki/Sexual_objectification#Sexual_objectification_of_women" title="Sexual objectification">female objectification</a> in the rap industry."<sup id="cite_ref-dazeed_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dazeed-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> For <i><a href="/wiki/NME" title="NME">NME</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s editor Jordan Bassett, she "out-filthed the male rappers at every turn."<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/BBC_News" title="BBC News">BBC News</a>, <i>Hard Core</i> (1996) was the most successful debut album by a female hip hop artist at the time; the album saw her "inhabit the territory of sexual edginess and hardcore rap, traditionally the preserve inhabited by male artists."<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_220-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> In 2000, <i><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">Los Angeles Times</a></i> writer Isaac Guzman wrote that her debut solo album created a path for a new wave of female rappers, with her at the forefront of her generation, and established her as "the raunchiest, hardest, sexiest, most glamorous woman in all of hip-hop."<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During that time, several new female hip hop artists modeled themselves after her stylistically.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> Placing <i>Hard Core</i> at number 80 on their list of "Best Rap Albums of the 90s," <i><a href="/wiki/Complex_(magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Complex (magazine)">Complex</a></i> magazine stated, "when Lil Kim released her debut album—women in hip-hop finally had options, paths to follow and models [...] she was the raunchiest woman you ever heard on the mic."<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Village_Voice" title="The Village Voice">The Village Voice</a></i> writer <a href="/wiki/Joan_Morgan_(American_author)" title="Joan Morgan (American author)">Joan Morgan</a> contended that the album "transformed her into a <a href="/wiki/Cultural_icon" title="Cultural icon">cultural icon</a>," opining that it put an emphasis on sex appeal, looks, and packaging as a priority for female rappers.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Camera_Obscura_(journal)" title="Camera Obscura (journal)">Camera Obscura</a></i> listed her as one of the late twentieth-century "self-marketers of female trouble" in music, along with <a href="/wiki/Janet_Jackson" title="Janet Jackson">Janet Jackson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tina_Turner" title="Tina Turner">Tina Turner</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Aside from music, Lil' Kim is also known for a risk-taking and luxurious approach to fashion that has inspired many artists. She has been named as a fashion icon by various media outlets.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> <i>Vogue</i> editor-at-large <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Leon_Talley" title="André Leon Talley">André Leon Talley</a> called her "the black <a href="/wiki/Madonna" title="Madonna">Madonna</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-newsweek_219-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newsweek-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> Designers such as <a href="/wiki/Marc_Jacobs" title="Marc Jacobs">Marc Jacobs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Versace" title="Versace">Versace</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Armani" title="Giorgio Armani">Giorgio Armani</a> have credited Lil' Kim as an inspiration.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In 2020, <i><a href="/wiki/XXL_(magazine)" title="XXL (magazine)">XXL</a></i> wrote that "twenty-five years later, no rapper is more influential to the new female rhymers."<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some artists who have named Kim as influence include <a href="/wiki/Rihanna" title="Rihanna">Rihanna</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cardi_B" title="Cardi B">Cardi B</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Megan_Thee_Stallion" title="Megan Thee Stallion">Megan Thee Stallion</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kash_Doll" title="Kash Doll">Kash Doll</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bree_Runway" title="Bree Runway">Bree Runway</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Teyana_Taylor" title="Teyana Taylor">Teyana Taylor</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Doja_Cat" title="Doja Cat">Doja Cat</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rosal%C3%ADa" title="Rosalía">Rosalía</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hitmaka" title="Hitmaka">Hitmaka</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-influence_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-influence-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Casanova_(rapper)" title="Casanova (rapper)">Casanova</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-influence_239-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-influence-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Spice_(musician)" title="Spice (musician)">Spice</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-influence_239-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-influence-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zaytoven" title="Zaytoven">Zaytoven</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-influence_239-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-influence-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stefflon_Don" title="Stefflon Don">Stefflon Don</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Flo_Milli" title="Flo Milli">Flo Milli</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nicki_Minaj" title="Nicki Minaj">Nicki Minaj</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ivorian_Doll" title="Ivorian Doll">Ivorian Doll</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cupcakke" title="Cupcakke">Cupcakke</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Saweetie" title="Saweetie">Saweetie</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ice_Spice" title="Ice Spice">Ice Spice</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> <a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iggy_Azalea" title="Iggy Azalea">Iggy Azalea</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ms_Banks" title="Ms Banks">Ms Banks</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kash_Doll" title="Kash Doll">Kash Doll</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jhen%C3%A9_Aiko" title="Jhené Aiko">Jhené Aiko</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/City_Girls" title="City Girls">City Girls</a><sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Erica_Banks" title="Erica Banks">Erica Banks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> Actress <a href="/wiki/Taraji_P._Henson" title="Taraji P. Henson">Taraji P. Henson</a> said that Lil' Kim inspired her portrayal of <i><a href="/wiki/Empire_(2015_TV_series)" title="Empire (2015 TV series)">Empire</a></i> character <a href="/wiki/Cookie_Lyon" title="Cookie Lyon">Cookie Lyon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_nominations">Awards and nominations</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_awards_and_nominations_received_by_Lil%27_Kim" title="List of awards and nominations received by Lil&#39; Kim">List of awards and nominations received by Lil' Kim</a></div>
<p>Throughout her career, Lil' Kim has earned numerous honors and awards. Her debut <a href="/wiki/Hard_Core_(Lil%27_Kim_album)" title="Hard Core (Lil&#39; Kim album)"><i>Hard Core</i></a> was certified double platinum by the RIAA, and is considered a classic hip-hop album.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> As of 2007, Lil' Kim has sold over 15 million albums worldwide. Her songs "No Time," "Big Momma Thang" and "Ladies Night" were listed on <a href="/wiki/Complex_(magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Complex (magazine)">Complex Magazine's</a> "The 50 Best Rap Songs By Women," at number 24,  13, and 7, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-The_50_Best_Rap_Songs_by_Women_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_50_Best_Rap_Songs_by_Women-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Her song "Lady Marmalade" with Pink, Christina Aguilera, and Mýa, is one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_singles" title="List of best-selling singles">best-selling singles of all time worldwide</a>, with sales of over 5.1 million worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> <i>Lady Marmalade</i> went number one on the <a href="/wiki/US_Billboard_Hot_100" class="mw-redirect" title="US Billboard Hot 100">US <i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a>, making her one of only a few female rappers to have a number one on that chart. The song won a 2002 <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Grammy Award">Grammy Award</a> in the category of "<a href="/wiki/Best_Pop_Collaboration_with_Vocals" class="mw-redirect" title="Best Pop Collaboration with Vocals">Best Pop Collaboration with Vocals</a>."
</p><p>Lil' Kim was ranked as one of the top 50 greatest MCs of all time in <a href="/wiki/Kool_Moe_Dee" title="Kool Moe Dee">Kool Moe Dee</a>'s 2003 book, <i><a href="/wiki/There%27s_a_God_on_the_Mic" title="There&#39;s a God on the Mic">There's a God on the Mic</a>.</i><sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> In 2012, Lil' Kim was honorably listed on VH1's "100 Greatest Women In Music" at number 45, placing her in the fourth highest position for a female rapper.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> In 2014, she was included in <i>Billboard</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s list of the "31 Female Rappers Who Changed Hip-Hop."<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> In 2016, VH1 honored Lil' Kim with VH1 Hip-Hop Honors and WEEN Award honored Kim with Icon Award because of her contribution to hip hop.
</p><p>In June 2018, Lil' Kim was honored with a proclamation by the New York City Council's Black, Latino, and Asian Caucus (BLAC) as part of the annual <a href="/wiki/African-American_Music_Appreciation_Month" title="African-American Music Appreciation Month">African-American Music Appreciation Month</a> for her contributions to hip-hop. Along with the proclamation, she also accepted the New York City 12th Council District Arts and Music Award and the city's Power Of Influence Award.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> In 2019, Lil' Kim was honored by <a href="/wiki/BET_Hip_Hop_Awards" title="BET Hip Hop Awards">BET Hip Hop Awards</a> with the I Am Hip Hop Award.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-dazeed-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-dazeed_4-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-dazeed_4-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFHall" class="citation news cs1">Hall, Jake. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.dazeddigital.com/music/article/33734/1/why-lil-kim-s-hard-core-is-as-relevant-as-ever">"Lil Kim - Music/Radio - Nairaland"</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 9,</span> 2017</span>. <q>Kim was, however, the first high-profile female rapper to flip the script on female objectification in the rap industry. <li id="cite_note-:2-229"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-:2_229-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFIandoli" class="citation news cs1">Iandoli, Kathy. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://theboombox.com/lil-kim-hard-core/">"10 Reasons Why Lil' Kim's 'Hard Core' Is Good for Hip-Hop"</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 9,</span> 2017</span>. <q>Kim's contribution to rap with her first LP left a lasting impression on rap music as a whole — one that still echoes today.Say what you will about the present situation between Lil' Kim and Nicki Minaj, but Kim's 'Hard Core' mothered an entire generation of the female rappers that followed. By discussing sexuality in an unabashed style, upping tax brackets with lavish lifestyles and sitting in the same thrones men dominated in hip-hop for years, 'Hard Core' opened up doors.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.atitle=10+Reasons+Why+Lil%27+Kim%27s+%27Hard+Core%27+Is+Good+for+Hip-Hop&amp;rft.aulast=Iandoli&amp;rft.aufirst=Kathy&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Ftheboombox.com%2Flil-kim-hard-core%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALil%27+Kim" class="Z3988"></span></span>
